 Fig. 13  (a) Schematic of a novel GDE-based cell with a thin layer of catholyte buffer (green area) and a bipolar membrane (BPM) for efficient formate production (HCOO−). Reproduced with permission.301 Copyright 2020, American Chemical Society; (b) formate concentration, FE, and energy efficiency of flow-cell CO2RR directly feeding a formate fuel cell. Reproduced with permission.289 Copyright 2020, The Royal Society of Chemistry; (c) FE of 2D bismuth-loaded GDE as a function of current in H-cell and flow-cell configurations. Reproduced with permission.303 Copyright 2018, Wiley; (d) bismuth (Bi) nanosheet directly electrodeposited on carbon-based GDL. Reproduced with permission.196 Copyright 2019, Elsevier; (e) dependence of formic acid product concentration on the N2 gas flow rate under a fixed overall current density of 200 mA cm−2. Utilizing dry N2 gas flow, up to nearly 100 wt% pure formic acid was achieved; (f) schematic of an all-vapor-phase operation CO2 electrolyzer to produce high-purity formic acid (AEM = anion exchange membrane, CEM = cation exchange membrane, PSE = porous solid electrolyte). Reproduced with permission.304 Copyright 2020, Springer Nature. 
Maximizing formate concentration facilitates its direct utilization in a closed loop of ‘electricity–formate–electricity’, which is a promising approach towards sustainable CO2RR to formate. Recently, Xiang et al. directly used produced formate from CO2RR in a fuel cell to generate power.289 Formate (0.5 M) was produced after 1h of CO2RR at a current density of 251 mA cm−2 in an alkaline electrolyte flow electrolyzer, fabricated with a GDE loaded with commercial SnO2 (supported on carbon black). A formate production yield of 22 g L−1 was achieved after 1 h operation (Fig. 11b), one of the highest reported for GDE-based electrolyzers. This high rate was due to the synergy between the alkaline electrolyte and GDE. Direct feeding of the product to a formate fuel cell produced a peak power density of 92 mW cm−2. This high formate production rate was further improved using 2D SnO2 nanosheets as the electrocatalyst in the GDE.292 Due to the significantly large surface area of thin nanosheets (3 nm in thickness), Li et al. achieved a new record for formate production among monometallic Sn-based catalysts at a large current density of 471 mA cm−2 with 94.2% FE. These recent high production rates show the enormous potential for energy production via CO2 reduction to formate with direct utilization.
GDEs for formate production mainly use Sn (tin) electrocatalyst; however, other promising electrocatalysts such as Bi (bismuth), In (indium) or CuSn alloy are being explored.305–308 Wang et al. recently showed that FE for formate with an Sn-loaded GDE could be improved via alloying with Cu.309 This is due to stronger adsorption of the HCOO* intermediate (required to produce formate) on CuSn intermetallic regions, leading to the suppression of HER. Bi-loaded GDEs are also attracting much attention because Bi needs a smaller overpotential as compared with Sn to produce formic acid.310 While metallic Bi has a formate FE of around 70%, De Arquer et al. boosted its performance by selectively enriching highly active Bi (Bi (110) facets) via preparation of the 2D nanosheets from BiOBr template, resulting in much higher current density compared to Bi (Fig. 13c).303 As a result, the GDE prepared with this 2D Bi-based catalyst performed stably over 65 h, reaching a record FE value of 90% with over 200 mA cm−2 current density in non-alkaline electrolyte (2 M KHCO3) (Fig. 13d). The modification of electrocatalyst chemical structure and resultant catalytic active defects, significantly improves GDE efficiency.254,255,306 Wang et al. directly electrodeposited uniform Bi nanosheets on a carbon-based GDL (Fig. 13e) and achieved up to 91% FE for formate.196 Through nanostructure tailoring, nanosheets of Bi303 and SnO2292 improve GDE CO2RR performance. In particular, nanosheets present abundant corner and edge sites, which facilitate strong local electric fields and contribute to the selective reduction of CO2 to formate.194,311
3.6 Recent advances in GDEs for CO2 reduction to CO (carbon monoxide)
Carbon monoxide is a major product of CO2RR, produced through a 2-electron transfer reaction. This process is of great importance as CO can be further converted to various hydrocarbons via the Fischer–Tropsch process or CORR.64 In addition, if the ratio of CO and H2 production is tuned precisely, syngas can be directly produced from CO2RR.316–318 Silver (Ag) and gold (Au) are the most-studied electrocatalysts for fabricating GDEs with an FE of 90–100% for CO.274 To date, large-scale application has been limited by the cost of these catalysts; therefore, maximizing catalyst efficiency is a priority.

3.6.1. Improving Ag/Au utilization in GDEs. 
A newly developed method for GDE preparation via MOF-mediated synthesis (Metal–Organic Frameworks) showed good CO2RR to CO performance with 385 mA cm−2 current density, over 96% FE, and only 0.2 mg cm−2 of Ag loading.319 In this method, a silver coordination polymer is built on porous support via multiple layer deposition, and subsequently electro-decomposited resulting in a highly uniform Ag-based CL on the GDE, schematically depicted in Fig. 14a. MOF-mediated synthesis proved more efficient than conventional CL deposition methods such as drop-casting, resulting in a catalyst layer (Fig. 14b and c) which minimized exposure of the electrolyte to substrate, avoiding HER. In addition, a high mass activity was recorded (1864–1926 mA mgAg−1), which is an important step towards minimizing costs where precious metal catalysts are utilized.
 Fig. 14  (a) Schematic of Ag-loaded GDE preparation via MOF-mediated layer-by-layer method; SEM images of Ag-loaded GDEs prepared via (b) conventional drop-casting, (c) MOF-mediated layer-by-layer method shown in (a). Reproduced with permission.319 Copyright 2019, American Chemical Society; (d) schematic of CL modification with CNT (MWCNT = multi-walled carbon nanotubes). Reproduced with permission.320 Copyright 2016, The Royal Society of Chemistry; (e) TiO2 as the catalyst holder to fabricate a GDE for efficient CO2RR to CO. Reproduced with permission.325 Copyright 2014, Wiley. 
In another study, Ma et al. incorporated multi-walled carbon nanotubes (MWCNT) to act as a conductive scaffold for Ag nanoparticles, owing to the van der Waals forces and high length-to-diameter ratio of MWCNT (Fig. 14d).320 By doing this, an Ag loading of 0.2 mg cm−2 was achieved for the Ag + MWCNT CL (Fig. 14d, ES3). It achieved a higher current density (350 mA cm−2), and the same FE of CO, as two other orientations (Fig. 14d, ES1 and ES2) with 1 mg cm−2 Ag loading. Incorporation of MWCNT improved the CO2 diffusion rate with microchannels, and reduced the charge transfer resistance within the CL;321 therefore, ES2 and ES3 configurations both had a higher current density than ES1 (Fig. 14d). The CL was prepared via drop-casting of Ag + MWCNT in ethanol without ionomer. This is beneficial as ionomers can potentially cover and deactivate the catalysts. Silver can also be loaded on MWCNT274 or support such as TiO2, assisting CO2 adsorption, decreasing overpotential by stabilizing the CO2− intermediate, and acting as a redox electron carrier to facilitate CO2RR to CO (Fig. 14e).322,323 Using TiO2 offers a few advantages: (1) TiO2 is 5–10 times cheaper than Ag, and (2) Super-fine Ag particles (sub-10 nm) can be loaded onto TiO2. It is known that electrocatalysts with diameter less than 10 nm have substantially better catalytic performance than larger particles, but without support, are susceptible to agglomeration.238,324 The GDE loaded with Ag–TiO2 catalyst showed very similar performance to the unsupported Ag-loaded GDE, with 2.5 times less Ag consumed, and over 90% FE for CO with a current density over 100 mA cm−2.325
Combining a low loading of Au with high current density for CO2 reduction to CO, a recent GDE loaded with Au clusters (Au25(SR)18) has shown exceptional activity for CO formation with both concentrated and diluted CO2 streams.326 The GDE prepared with Au clusters (Fig. 15a) allowed substantially lower Au loading at 16 μg cm−2 as compared with Au nanoparticles loaded on carbon black with 0.39 mg cm−2 loading. The Au clusters also displayed higher- and very stable selectivity for CO in 10% CO2 gas stream, which is the typical CO2 concentration in flue gas (Fig. 15b). This suggests that an energy-intensive CO2 purification step may be unnecessary, provided the electrocatalyst has high sensitivity to adsorb CO2 from the dilute stream. In this case, the GDE performance is not affected by low CO2 concentration in the vicinity of the electrocatalyst.327 Gold clusters have electron-deficient and low-coordinate atoms, which set them apart from Au nanoparticles. Thus, extraordinary current densities up to 540 mA cm−2 can be achieved when Au is used in a GDE. In addition, these porous-structure electrocatalysts help to mitigate bubble formation on the GDE surface.
 Fig. 15  (a) Schematic of Au clusters and nanoparticles loaded on carbon black (C) to fabricate a GDE for CO production; (b) CO production selectivity on Au clusters and Au nanoparticles at −0.36 V vs. RHE as a function of CO2 partial pressure. Reproduced with permission.326 Copyright 2019, American Chemical Society; (c) schematic of CO bubble formation on GDE surface, interrupting the catalyst-electrolyte interface (inactive reaction sites); (d) cell voltage instability at various current densities (5–80 mA cm−2), and severe cell voltage fluctuation at high current densities (60–80 mA cm−2). Reproduced with permission.279 Copyright 2020, Elsevier; (e) GDE fabricated from non-conductive PVDF nano-fibers by composite deposition of Cu and Sn. PVDF is coated by polydopamine (pDA). Cu and Sn are deposited on pDA layer to form a Cu/Sn alloy catalyst layer. Reproduced with permission.332 Copyright 2019, Wiley; (f) electrochemical spontaneous precipitation (ESP) method to fabricate Cu/In-loaded GDE. Reproduced with permission.262 Copyright 2020, American Chemical Society. 
In production of gaseous products (such as CO or ethylene), bubble accumulation is an issue as it interferes with the electrolyte-catalyst reaction interfaces by covering the CL (Fig. 15c).328 This phenomenon increases the ohmic resistance and triggers voltage instability which is more likely to occur at higher current densities with higher formation rates for the gaseous products (Fig. 15d).279 Loading a CL with a sharp-structured electrocatalyst, e.g. nanoneedles or nanorods, is an effective way to mitigate bubble accumulation, and to increase the active surface area to minimize the impacts of slight bubble formation. These catalyst morphologies reduce the time required for nucleation and release of the bubbles, therefore bubbles depart the CL at a much smaller size, and do not grow on the catalyst surface.122


3.6.2. GDEs with non-noble electrocatalysts. 
GDE-based CO2RR studies to produce CO have mainly focused on using Au and Ag due to their high conversion selectivity.274 However, it is more economical to consider earth-abundant options to reduce the cost of GDE preparation. Catalysts made from non-noble metals such as alloys of Cu/Sn and Cu/In offer promising performance for CO2RR to CO;170,246,329–331 however, their GDE-based performance is yet to be widely evaluated. Alloy powders can be used to make the CL ink for conventional GDE fabrication,309 but other methods are being developed offering improved control over the alloy ratio, the key to tuning product selectivity.
Ju et al. used non-conductive electrospun polyvinylidene fluoride (PVDF) nanofibers to fabricate a Cu/Sn alloy GDE.332 Nanofibers were coated with polydopamine to provide nuclei for electroless Cu deposition to make PVDF conductive, prior to Sn electrodeposition on the Cu layer forming the alloy (Fig. 15e). Using the electrodeposition duration to carefully control the amount of Sn deposited, a CO partial current density above 100 mA cm−2 was achieved with over 80% FE. The CO partial current density was improved almost ten-fold, as compared with other Sn-deposited Cu planar catalysts,239,330 owing to enhanced CO2 mass transport using the GDE. Recently, Xiang et al. used a novel method to fabricate a GDE with bimetallic Cu/In for CO2RR to CO via electrochemical spontaneous precipitation (ESP, Fig. 15f).262,263 Combining a GDE flow cell with strongly alkaline electrolyte (1 M KOH), achieved a current density of ∼200 mA cm−2 with over 90% FE of CO. EPS consumes zero-energy, simply comprising immersion of a Cu2O-coated GDE in an In3+ solution, resulting in a spontaneously formed In layer, tunable by immersion duration. These recent improvements in methods to develop GDEs have lowered the Ag/Au loading significantly. In addition, the use of Cu/Sn and Cu/In alloys262,309,332 has reduced the dependency on expensive Ag/Au for selective CO production. A summary of GDEs fabricated for CO2 reduction to CO can be found in Table 2.

3.7. Recent advances in GDEs for CO2 reduction to hydrocarbons (methane, ethylene) and alcohols (ethanol, methanol, and propanol)
In addition to CO and formic acid, other valuable chemicals can also be produced using Cu-based electrocatalysts with appreciable selectivity towards C2+ products.54,195,247,260,341–343 The current section focuses on CO2RR for production of methane, methanol, and other C2+ products such as ethylene, ethanol, and propanol.344 Synthesis of these products is challenging due to multiple proton-electron transfers, requiring selective electrocatalysts and modulation of CO2 concentration via CO2 flow operational conditions or CL porosity/thickness.123,345 Because CO is the critical intermediate in formation of these products, optimal levels of CO should be made available on the catalyst surface. While much GDE CO2RR research has focused on Cu-based electrocatalysts for the synthesis of these products, single product selectivity remains a major challenge.

3.7.1. Production of methane. 
Selective formation of methane from CO2 is uncommon since methane formation is suppressed on Cu-based electrocatalysts under alkaline conditions, similar to HER (local pH of GDE is alkaline as shown in Fig. 9b).192,198 Methane is the main product when electrolysis takes place at a lower temperature (e.g. 0 °C) using specific Cu-based catalysts such as polymer-supported Cu–Pd nanoalloy,346 Cu–porphyrin complex,347 or nano-Cu/glassy carbon catalyst.348 The preparation of these catalysts is complicated, with low feasibility for scale-up. Recently, Zhang et al. fabricated a GDE loaded with a highly dispersed, single-site Cu–N catalyst for the electrochemical reduction of CO2 to methane in a flow cell reactor with 1 M KOH electrolyte.349 With a methane partial current density of 100 mA cm−2 (methane/ethylene ratio of 4:1), this study showed that limited size Cu nanoparticles favor methane production by suppressing the C–C coupling step required for ethylene production.348 Wang et al. recently found that lowering CO2 coverage on the CL is the key to high FE of methane from CO2RR on a Cu-based GDE, favoring the protonation of *CO to *CHO, the main intermediate for methane generation.350 Feeding diluted CO2 (75% CO2) to the GDE achieved the highest-reported partial current density of 108 mA cm−2 for methane. Further research is required to synthesize methane-selective electrocatalysts for CO2RR under alkaline or locally alkaline conditions, and to tune methane selectivity using operational variables.


3.7.2. Production of C2+ hydrocarbons and alcohols. 
For C2+ products, a GDE prepared with a nanoporous Cu catalyst with pore size 100–200 nm exhibited an excellent high partial current density of 400 mA cm−2 and FE of 62%.273 The nanoporous CL offered abundant catalytic active sites, and resulted in one of the highest current densities for C2+ products with good stability after 2 h electrolysis. During high-rate electrolysis, managing the transport of gaseous products through the GDE is challenging as bubble accumulation can trigger voltage instability, and disturb gas and ion diffusion pathways to/away from the catalytic sites, or block the catalytic active sites, as seen in Fig. 15c and d.279 Nanoporous Cu offers the desired porosity (Fig. 16a), provided that the catalyst loading is adjusted carefully. A high catalyst loading results in increased gaseous product accumulation, which leads to a drop in performance (0.8 mg cm−2 was the optimum loading in this study). This highly porous CL also enhances the catalyst surface accessibility, and prevents the negative effect of carbonation at the electrode–electrolyte interface, which degrades performance, as previously explained.274
 Fig. 16  (a) Cross-sectional SEM image of the nano-porous copper catalyst on GDL. Reproduced with permission.273 Copyright 2018, Wiley; (b) FE of C2+ products under various applied potentials (−0.2 to −0.9 V vs. RHE) over halogen–Cu catalyst GDEs; (c) Formation rates of C2+ products under various applied potentials over halogen–Cu catalyst GDEs in 1 M KOH. Reproduced with permission.124 Copyright 2020, Springer Nature; This study shows the highest rate and FE for C2+ products; (d) schematic of the Cu-loaded GDE with polymer intrinsic microporosity layer (PIM) improving ethylene production performance. Reproduced with permission.354 Copyright 2020, Elsevier; (e) schematic of the layered structure of the tandem GDE with a ZnO layer over a Cu layer; (f) combined layer of Cu + ZnO as the CL. Reproduced with permission.356 Copyright 2020, Elsevier. 
For ethylene and C2+ products, the novel ionomer-treated PTFE-based GDEs mentioned in Fig. 12a and b, resulted in one of the highest current density with over 900 and 1200 mA cm−2 for ethylene and C2+, respectively, with stability for over 150 h.28 However, a recent series of GDEs with halogen-modified Cu electrocatalysts demonstrated a record-breaking current density of 1.6 A cm−2 with 80% FE for ethanol and ethylene.124 With almost seven-fold higher C2+ formation rate and doubled FE as compared with a GDE with unmodified (bare) Cu (Fig. 16b and c), this study revealed that molecular modification of Cu substantially improves GDE performance. It has been shown that facile doping of fluorine or sulfur can greatly enhance the catalytic activity and selectivity of the catalysts.254,255,351,352 If ethanol is the single product of interest, deoxygenation and the production of ethylene should be suppressed. Wang et al. recently reported 52% FE of ethanol for a Cu-based GDE coated by a nitrogen-doped carbon (N–C) layer.353 Necessary for ethanol formation, the (N–C) layer has a strong electron-donating ability and can suppress breaking of the C–O bond.
Inspired by the ionomer treatment method, (Fig. 12a) establishing a high CO2 concentration on the GDE surface and preventing bubble accumulation, Perry et al. coated the CL with a layer of polymer with intrinsic microporosity (PIM) (Fig. 16d).354 This drop-casted PIM thin layer is permeable to the electrolyte, and on contact, a CO2-saturated layer of electrolyte is formed on the Cu-based CL. Therefore, CO2 is stored in a triphasic environment for facile reduction, improving the efficiency of CO2 reduction to ethylene, provided that the thickness of hydrophilic PIM is optimized to avoid a negative impact on the water-repellence of the GDE. This design showed high surface hydrophobicity stability with prolonged operation, and corresponding improved stability of ethylene production.355
Another new CL design to improve the hydrocarbon selectivity of CO2RR integrates Cu with a CO-generation catalyst to fabricate a ‘mixed catalyst’ CL. For example, ZnO, in the CL can enhance the C–C coupling kinetics by increasing the local concentration of key intermediate, CO. Zhang et al. tried this by designing a Cu/ZnO tandem GDE, as shown in Fig. 16e.356 The ZnO layer feeds CO to the Cu layer, maintains a high concentration of CO for further reduction to hydrocarbons, and increases the CO utilization efficiency. This tandem design showed improved performance with current density and FE of C2+ products, 1.8 and 1.3 times, respectively, higher than that of the mixed Cu + ZnO GDE, as shown in Fig. 16f. The main concept behind the tandem design is that the CO produced on the ZnO (or Ag layer) layer diffuses back to the Cu layer, effectively maintaining a high CO concentration on the GDE.357 This study introduced a new method of tuning C2+ product selectivity by considering a mixture/layers of different catalysts in the CL of a GDE, without any atomic- or nano-scale adjacent. It should be noted that for selective hydrocarbon production from CO2RR on a GDE, the Cu catalyst nanostructure and surface chemistry play critical roles.280 For example, a GDE fabricated with Cu nanocubes showed substantially better catalytic activity and selectivity for ethylene formation than a GDE made with Cu nanospheres.358 The Cu nanocubes, rich with (110) facets, have better C–C coupling to the intermediate *CO than (111) facets, enabling high activity for C2 products. In addition, Cu nanocrystals with a single-crystal nature have a similar catalytic activity to the highly polycrystalline and disordered catalysts with surface defects (e.g. high-angle grain boundaries, steps, and corners), thereby enhancing single product selectivity.108 Therefore, the selection of appropriate electrocatalyst in terms of the nanostructure, shape, and facet is necessary for optimal performance and selective production of hydrocarbons/alcohols from CO2RR.38,39,42,47,49,56,260
Fewer studies have focused on using a GDE-based electrolyzer to produce multicarbon liquid products such as methanol and propanol from CO2, than to produce ethylene and ethanol.341,359–361 Albo et al. used Cu2O- and Cu2O/ZnO-loaded GDEs to reduce CO2 to methanol,362–364 since Cu-oxide and oxide-derived Cu have shown better catalytic activity for CO2 reduction to C2+ products, as compared with metal Cu.365–367 Cu2O- and Cu2O/ZnO-loaded GDEs with FE of around 55% outperformed most Cu-based catalysts used for conversion of CO2 to methanol.363 Cu2O is prone to losing catalytic activity due to the reduction of Cu+ to Cu during the cathodic reaction. However, Cu2O/ZnO-loaded GDE exhibited superior stability as compared with the Cu2O-based GDE, despite having a lower FE for methanol. Similarly, a GDE prepared with CuAg alloys performed well for high-rate C2+ formation due to the presence of Ag to stabilize Cu2O in the CL.195 Given the economic value of these products (especially propanol) from CO2RR, more studies are being carried out in GDE-based flow cells, focusing on modulating the selectivity of Cu via molecular modifications.195,368 As presented in Table 3, the highest selectivity recorded for CO2RR multicarbon products is for ethylene.124 Selective CO2 reduction to multicarbons apart from ethylene remains a challenge, mainly because the product selectivity of Cu catalyst decreases due to the conversion of CO2 to formate, CO, H2, and ethylene.38 In this regard, direct CO reduction (CORR) has exhibited better performance than CO2RR, as discussed in the following sections.

3.7.3. Effect of CO2 concentration and flow rate. 
In addition to electrocatalyst and CL design, gas feed concentration, and flow rate are two key operational parameters which enhance the formation of C2+ products on GDEs, by boosting C–C coupling through modulating the local CO2 concentration.345 As seen in Fig. 17a, with the dilution of CO2 feed to 50%, the required potentials to achieve a current density of 100 or 200 mA cm−2 were similar to those using pure gas. However, for 25% CO2 or less, the potential increased along with a higher FE of HER and a lower FE of C1 or C2+ products, due to the low local CO2 availability.123,369 Moreover, the 50% CO2 feed showed a substantially higher FE for C2+ at 200 mA cm−2 as compared with pure CO2. This is due to the lower *CO2 surface coverage which leads to more optimal coverage of *CO and other intermediates required to enhance FE of C2+ products. At 100 mA cm−2 (correspondent to a lower potential), FE of C2+ products remained constant, but the FE of C1 products was positively correlated to each respective CO2 feed concentration. This indicates the differing effects of CO2 concentration on C2+ and C1 products, and that the C–C coupling rate (which is essential for C2+ products formation) is more sensitive to the local CO2 concentration at higher current densities.
 Fig. 17  (a) FE of CO2RR products at various CO2 feed concentrations (25–100%) and 100, 200 mA cm−2 current densities; (b) FE of CO2RR products at various CO2 feed flow rates (5–40 sccm or standard cubic centimeters per minute) and 100, 200 mA cm−2 current densities. Reproduced with permission.123 Copyright 2020, Elsevier; (c) FE of C2 products (ethylene and ethanol) for 15% CO2 (v/v) feed stream, with and without the presence of O2 at 1 bar and 15 bar in 1 M KOH as a function of cell voltage, as compared with 100% CO2 (v/v) at 1 bar; (d) stability of CO2RR to C2 products on a Cu-PTFE GDE coated with hydrophilic ionomer at 10 bar absolute pressure in 1 M KOH electrolyte, with feed gas containing 15% CO2 (v/v) and 4% O2 (v/v); (e) schematic of a Cu-PTFE GDE coated with a hydrophobic (left) or hydrophilic (right) nano-porous ionomer to minimize the adverse effects of O2 impurity. The hydrophilic layer slows O2 diffusion making the GDE O2-tolerant. Reproduced with permission.371 Copyright 2020, The Royal Society of Chemistry. 
Reducing the pure CO2 flow rate from 40 to 5 sccm increased the C2+ product FE exponentially to 60.3%, while the FE of C1 products exhibited the opposite trend (Fig. 17b). The potential and H2 FE remained relatively constant for various flow rates, indicating that CO2 availability was sufficient even at 5 sccm. A lower CO2 flow rate helps to optimize the local CO2 concentration for enhanced C–C coupling. Therefore, CO2 concentration and flowrate, in addition to the microstructure of the CL (as shown in Fig. 6d), need to be precisely regulated to tune the product selectivity of CO2RR to C1 or C2+ products.370 The importance of flow conditions has been underestimated in previous studies, and poor FE of multicarbon products from CO2RR can be attributed to the lack of optimized CO2 concentration/flow rate.
While regulating local CO2 concentration is necessary to tune the CO2RR selectivity, the capacity to use a diluted CO2 feed, similar to industrial gas streams such as flue gas with 15% CO2, is a necessary step to scale-up GDE-based electrolyzers without a CO2 pre-purification step. However, it was seen that dilution of the CO2 feed to less than 50% reduced the FE of C1 or C2+ products (Fig. 17a, 25% CO2 diluted feed).
Operating CO2RR at an elevated pressure can neutralize the negative effect of diluted CO2 feed (Fig. 17c). This is a more economical option than a CO2 purification step, and aids HER suppression.275,335 Therefore, a higher FE for C2 products from CO2RR was observed with simulated flue gas at 15 bar, as compared with pure CO2 at 1 bar (Fig. 17c, blue and black lines). However, the effect of O2 impurities in real industrial flue gas streams (4% v/v), and the competing ORR,372 which may consume up to 99% of the applied current, should not be overlooked.373 In addition, pressurization does not stop the parasitic ORR when O2 impurity exists (Fig. 17c, dashed line). One way to address this issue is by slowing O2 transport to the CL surface through a selective coating barrier on the GDE. For this purpose, O2 diffusion and adsorption on the CL can be avoided via an O2-repelling ionomer layer as schematically shown in Fig. 17e.371 The hydrophilic ionomer coating was able to impede O2 diffusion because when the hydrophilic nanopores are filled with electrolyte, O2 diffuses less readily than CO2 through the nanopores, due to the volatile nature of O2 in aqueous solutions. In addition, the hydrophobic barrier provides a non-selective gas-phase diffusive pathway for both CO2 and O2. The GDE with a hydrophilic coating can convert O2-containing dilute CO2 feeds with a FEC2 of 68% and over 10 hours of stable operation (Fig. 17d), comparable with successful CO2RR to C2 products generated with pure CO2 feed.192,195 In addition, as shown in Fig. 12 and 16d, a hydrophilic layer on the GDE can improve the GDE wettability and ion transport, and increase the FE of the desired product. A summary of the GDEs fabricated for CO2 reduction to hydrocarbons and alcohols is given in Table 3.

3.8. Key findings and challenges in GDE-based CO2RR
• Efficient GDE-based CO2RR with selective electrocatalysts for CO (Au, Ag) and formate (Sn, Bi) has been achieved.
• Selective GDE-based CO2RR to multicarbons besides ethylene remains a challenge as the product selectivity of Cu catalyst decreases due to the conversion of CO2 to formate, CO, H2, and ethylene.38
• Increasing the single-pass conversion of CO2 and purity of the gas/liquid products is critical to reducing the cost of CO2RR in real applications. Operating conditions such as CO2 flow/concentration should be optimized to maximize single-pass conversion. This is crucial for gas products which are often mixed with unreacted CO2.
• Carbonation and flooding are still major issues with using GDEs for CO2RR. Although ultra-high current densities have been achieved28,124 further studies are needed to confirm stability over time. In particular, new electrocatalysts should be assessed for at least 100 h. Long-term stability tests also are needed to determine electrocatalyst durability in terms of morphology and active sites.
• GDE stability should be assessed in real CO2 gas streams with impurities. Impurities not only decrease CO2 concentration in the proximity of the CL, but impurities can also interrupt CO2RR mechanisms or cause changes to the electrocatalyst surface chemistry. Therefore, more attention should be given to examining the durability of GDEs with feeds simulating real gas streams.
4. GDEs for carbon monoxide reduction reaction (CORR)
4.1. Advantages of GDE-based CORR over CO2RR
Carbon monoxide reduction reaction (CORR) is an early stage, promising electrocatalytic process, which solely uses Cu and Cu-based materials as electrocatalysts.64,150,379–382 CORR presents a huge economic opportunity because the conventional method of CO conversion i.e., the Fischer–Tropsch process, is energy-intensive and operated at high temperatures (200–400 °C) and pressure (up to 200 atm).383 CORR produces various multicarbon and oxygenated products,384 and exhibits similar pathways to CO2RR, after CO2 is converted to surface adsorbed CO (COads) as the first intermediate to multicarbon products.385,386 However, given the different properties of CO and CO2 when dissolved in an aqueous electrolyte, CORR holds several advantages over CO2RR, when designing an alkaline GDE-based electrolyzer to produce multicarbon products.178
As previously discussed, carbonation during CO2RR is a major issue and makes alkaline CO2RR fundamentally unsustainable.22,232,274,337 CORR can circumvent this issue, owing to the inherent stability of CO in alkaline conditions (Fig. 18a), and high selectivity of CORR to C2+ products.22,145,177,226,387 In addition, CO2 to formic acid requires fewer electrons (2e−), and is classified as a dead-end pathway in CO2RR, competing with multicarbon production.178,388 Moreover, for the same multicarbon product, less electron transfer is needed in CORR as compared with CO2RR, e.g., ethanol/ethylene production requires 12e− in CO2RR, and 8e− in CORR. Importantly, during alkaline CO2RR the catholyte pH drops, even at 0 mA cm−2, due to the rapid equilibrium reaction between CO2 and KOH (Fig. 18b).389 This local pH drop does not happen for CORR. At 300 mA cm−2, the OH− concentration under CORR conditions is one order of magnitude higher than under CO2RR, although the bulk pH is identical at 13.5 (Fig. 18b). Higher OH− concentration indicates lower ohmic resistance in close proximity to the CL and improved HER suppression.
 Fig. 18  (a) Schematic illustrating the CO2 interaction with OH− to form CO32−, and stability of CO in alkaline electrolyte. Reproduced with permission.22 Copyright 2019, Springer Nature; (b) pH profiles for CO2RR/CORR in 1 M KOH under various current densities as a function of distance from the CL (x = 0 means on the GDE surface); (c) cell voltage and CO2RR/CORR product distribution (bars) on an oxide-derived Cu-based GDE at 300 mA cm−2 in 1 M KOH; (d) cell voltage and product distribution of CORR (FE) in 2 M KOH at 500 mA cm−2 on an oxide-derived Cu-based GDE, EtOH = ethanol, PrOH = propanol, AcO− = acetic acid/acetate. Reproduced with permission.107 Copyright 2018, Springer Nature. 
These advantages highlight the superiority of CORR over CO2RR particularly for multicarbon production at high current densities (>200 mA cm−2).390–392 Direct CO feeding increases the near-surface concentration of CO, which is widely accepted to be a key reaction intermediate for the formation of C2+ products.150,178 A comparison of CO2RR and CORR using a Cu-based GDE in a 1 M KOH at 300 mA cm−2 revealed that the total FE of C2+ products from CORR (80%) was much higher than that from CO2RR (55%) (Fig. 18c, black circles). Moreover, in CO2RR over 20% of FE is dedicated to CO (15%) and formic acid (7%), which limits the FE of C2+ products.107,178 The overall cell voltage increased by ∼100 mV when the feed changed from CO to CO2, due to the additional energy required to activate CO2 relative to CO, and pH drop when CO2 was used. This, along with CORR lower electron demand for the same product, implies that less energy input is required for CORR over CO2RR. In addition, acetic acid as a techno-economically useful chemical,22,177 has a much lower FE in CO2RR than that in CORR (Fig. 18c). Furthermore, the GDE used for CO2RR showed a sharp increase in the cell voltage after 40 min, due to carbonate-derived flooding. In comparison, the CORR voltage was fairly stable with a slight increase (Fig. 18d), probably due to the build-up of gas bubbles at high current density.
4.2. Recent advances in GDEs for CORR

4.2.1. Production of ethylene. 
Han et al. recently for the first time used a Cu-based GDE for CORR (Fig. 19a), observing a significant increase in ethylene partial current density as compared with feeding CO via purging (Fig. 19b, increase from 0.9 to 50.8 mA cm−2).136 Following Han et al., several studies by Sargent et al. and Jiao et al. used GDEs for selective CORR to hydrocarbons by modifying the Cu nanostructure of the electrocatalyst.107,149,168,177,181,387 Recently, a record-breaking FE of 53% and a current density of >800 mA cm−2 were achieved in CO reduction to ethylene by constraining CO coverage on a Cu-loaded GDE using a mixture of CO and N2.168 Using pure CO on a Cu-loaded GDE usually results in an FE of up to 30% for ethylene in 1 M KOH (Fig. 18c and 19c);107 however, feeding 10% CO gas increased the FE to 53%. Therefore, the key to more selective ethylene production from CORR on a Cu-loaded GDE is to constrain operating conditions such that CO availability is neither too high (promotes oxygenates) nor too low (causes HER). A high concentration of CO leads to a higher density of *C2 intermediates at the catalyst–electrolyte interface, which subsequently react with hydroxide ions to form acetate, or are further reduced to C3 product, propanol (Fig. 19d right-side). An optimized CO concentration restricts further reduction of intermediates (Fig. 19d left-side), and ethylene selectivity can be effectively tuned. Using a diluted gas stream also increases performance, for example, a sharp increase from 25% to 75% in CO single-pass conversion was observed when 40% CO was used instead of pure CO, indicating that the majority of the pure CO stream exits the cell unreacted.226
 Fig. 19  (a) Fabrication procedure for a Cu-loaded GDE for CORR; (b) flow-through GDE-based and flow-by CO bubbling (non-GDE) for CORR to ethylene. Reproduced with permission.136 Copyright 2018, American Chemical Society; (c) the FEs of CORR products at various CO concentrations (2.5–100%) at an applied potential of −0.44 V vs. RHE in 1 M KOH; (d) schematic of porous GDE with CO reduction at the catalyst–electrolyte interface, showing how CO-deficiency and CO-surplus conditions influence product selectivity. Reproduced with permission.168 Copyright 2019, Springer Nature; (e) FEs of C2 and C3 products from CORR for Cu nanocatalysts of various morphologies (scale bar: 100 nm). Reproduced with permission.181 Copyright 2018, Springer Nature; (f) total current density and FEs of CORR products on a GDE with Cu nanosheets as the catalyst in 2 M KOH, as a function of the applied potential. Reproduced with permission.177 Copyright 2019, Springer Nature. 


4.2.2. Production of ethanol, acetate, and propanol. 
Ethylene is usually the dominant product of CORR if typical Cu particles are used as the GDE electrocatalyst (Fig. 18c and d). However, the production of C2+ products, such as ethanol, propanol, and acetate, is also feasible by CORR, in contrast to the inefficient selectivity of these products from CO2RR.178 In particular, products such as propanol and acetate are techno-economically appealing based on the commodity price and the average electricity price (assuming 2–3 V required cell voltage for electrolysis),22 although electrochemically produced ethanol at present cannot compete with the subsidized ethanol produced from corn.230 Unlike ethylene, whose FE can be improved via constraining CO, a high CO concentration on a Cu-based GDE is required to boost the oxygenation and formation of propanol/acetate (50–100% CO concentration, Fig. 19c).393 This explains the relatively higher FE of propanol/acetate in CORR than in CO2RR. Moreover, for C2+ products such as propanol, the nanostructure and active sites of Cu electrocatalyst need to be accurately tuned to promote the appropriate intermediates for the production of these hydrocarbons.180,394,395 For example, highly fragmented copper exhibiting a mixture of Cu(111) and Cu(100) facets selects for C1 and C2 intermediates, respectively, and these two intermediates are simultaneously required to convert CO to propanol, a liquid engine fuel.380,387
For propanol production, a GDE prepared with Cu nanoparticles with open cavities (as seen in Fig. 19e) showed superior FE as compared with solid nanoparticles or half-open cavities.181 It was observed that an open Cu nanocavity structure promotes C3 formation via the nanoconfinement of C2 intermediates, leading to an FE of propanol twice that achieved with solid particles. The highest documented FE of propanol from CORR has been 23%, obtained using copper ‘adparticles’. Adparticles possess a high density of undercoordinated atoms, which serve as preferential sites for n-propanol formation.394 For acetate production, the highest partial current density of 131 mA cm−2 was achieved with a 2D nanosheet Cu-loaded GDE (Fig. 19f).177 This was due to the exitance of Cu(111) steps, instead of Cu (100) and Cu(110), which effectively suppressed the formation of ethanol and propanol, and led to a higher FE of acetate as compared with spherical Cu particles. Therefore, to precisely tune the product selectivity of CORR, careful consideration should be given to the molecular structure and nanostructure of Cu electrocatalysts used in a GDE.46


4.2.3. Effect of CO concentration and flow rate. 
Increasing the single-pass conversion of CO is an important engineering requirement, as a low feed conversion strongly affects the product separation- and overall process cost.217 This can be achieved by reducing CO concentration in the feed and as can be seen in Fig. 20a, by using 40% CO flow, CO conversion substantially increases from around 20% to over 60%.226 Besides CO concentration, another operational parameter influencing GDE-based CORR is the CO flow rate. At a lower CO flux (0.125 vs. 0.5 cm3 min−1), a higher overpotential (by 70 mV) was required to attain a current density of 144 mA cm−2 (−1.49 V vs. −1.56 V, Fig. 20b and c).226 This means that more energy input is required for a given current density. Thus, the cell becomes less energy-efficient, resulting from insufficient CO supply. In addition, at a lower CO flux, HER becomes dominant at higher potentials/current densities (Fig. 20c, black bar). As the supplied CO is insufficient to maintain the rate of CORR to hydrocarbons on the GDE, the current is consumed by HER. In summary, the CO stream concentration and flow should be adjusted based on the applied potential (or current density) for the desired product, to achieve both high CO single-pass conversion and high FE. A summary of GDEs fabricated for CO reduction to various products can be found in Table 4.
 Fig. 20  (a) Product distributions and single-pass CO conversions as a function of CO concentration in the inlet gas stream with 1 M NaOH catholyte; partial current density of CORR product distributions as a function of GDE potentials with (b) 0.5 sccm CO flux; (c) 0.125 sccm CO flux. Reproduced with permission.226 Copyright 2019, Elsevier.
